The Prohibition of Intercourse with a Menstruating Wife and Its Exceptions
Ruling prohibiting intercourse with a menstruating woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intercourse with a menstruating woman, identify its Quranic proof, and explain the conditions for permissible intimacy and the recommended expiation.

Core Idea
Intercourse with a menstruating woman is haram; only limited intimacy with a barrier is allowed, and expiation is recommended if transgression occurs.

This lesson examines the clear prohibition of sexual intercourse during menstruation derived from the Quranic command to 'keep away' (fa'tazilu). The text not only establishes the prohibition but also provides a permissible alternative for maintaining closeness and outlines a recommended expiation if the prohibition is violated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage prohibits the husband from having intercourse with his menstruating wife. The ruling is haram (prohibited).
The prohibition is based on a clear Quranic verse.
2
Locate the Quranic dalil
The author cites Surah al-Baqarah 2:222, which calls menstruation 'adha' (harm) and commands avoidance until purification.
The imperative 'fa'tazilu' indicates prohibition.
3
Analyse the illa (effective cause)
The verse mentions 'adha' as the reason for prohibition. Hanafi usul classifies this as a zanni dalala (probable meaning) but the ruling remains qat'i due to Quranic text.
The illa is the harm (physical and ritual impurity).
4
Note the exception for intimacy
Despite the prohibition of intercourse, the husband may lie with his wife if she wears an izar (waist wrapper) as a barrier.
This shows that not all physical contact is forbidden.
5
Identify the expiation ruling
If intercourse occurs, expiation is recommended: one dinar at the beginning of menstruation, half a dinar at the end. This is based on a narration from Ibn Abbas, considered nawadir (weak) but still praiseworthy.
Hanafi mufta bihi: kaffarah is mustahabb, not wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۰۲] (۵) شوہر حائضہ بیوی سے وہی نہیں کرے گا۔
آیت میں ہے ویسئلونك عن المحيض قل هو اذى فاعتزلوا النساء في المحيض ولا تقربوهن حتى يطهرن (و) (آیت ۲۲۲ سورة البقرة ٢) نوٹ ولی تو کرنا حرام ہے۔ البتہ عورت کو ازار پہنا کر لیٹ سکتا ہے اور اگر اول حیض میں وطی کر لیا تو ایک دینار صدقہ کرے اور اخیر حیض میں ولی کر لیا تو آدھا دینار صدقہ کرے ( ابو داؤد باب فی ایتان الحائض ص ۴۰ نمبر ۲۶۵) میں حضرت ابن عباس کا قول ہے۔ اذا اصابها فى اول الدم فدينار واذا اصابها في انقطاع الدم فنصف دينار.
(5) The husband will not have intercourse with his menstruating wife. In the verse: 'And they ask you about menstruation. Say: It is harm, so keep away from women during menstruation and do not approach them until they become pure.' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:222). Note: Intercourse is haram. However, he can lie with her wearing an iza (waist wrapper). And if he has intercourse at the beginning of menstruation, he must give one dinar in charity; if at the end, half a dinar. (Abu Dawud, chapter on approaching menstruating woman, p.40 no.265) – the saying of Ibn Abbas: 'If he has intercourse in the beginning of the blood, then one dinar; if in the cessation, then half a dinar.'
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage confirms the prohibition of sexual intercourse with a menstruating woman, based on the clear Quranic verse calling menstruation 'harm' (adha) and commanding avoidance until purification. The author then addresses permissible physical intimacy: the husband may lie with his wife if she covers herself with a waist wrapper (izar). Additionally, a hadith from Ibn Abbas outlines expiation (kaffarah) if intercourse occurs: one dinar for early menstruation, half a dinar for later. This ruling is specific to Hanafi fiqh, based on a weak hadith but accepted by some Hanafi scholars as recommended charity.

Key Terms
اذى (adha): Harm, impurity, something unpleasant; the reason for prohibition.
ازار (izar): A waist wrapper or lower garment used to cover private parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One dinar
B) Half a dinar
C) One meal
D) No expiation

Key Takeaway
Intercourse with a menstruating woman is haram; only limited intimacy with a barrier is allowed, and expiation is recommended if transgressed.

Prohibition of Quran Recitation for Those in Major Impurity
Ruling prohibiting Quran recitation for those in major impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Quran recitation for menstruating women and junub, cite the primary hadith evidence, and identify the exception for teaching children.

Core Idea
Menstruating women and junub cannot recite Quran, but may break verses for teaching and engage in dhikr.

This lesson examines the prohibition of Quran recitation for those in a state of major ritual impurity (janabah and hayd). We will explore the primary hadith evidence, the Hanafi position, and a practical exception for teaching. Understanding this ruling helps maintain the sanctity of the Quran while accommodating necessary situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Recognize that the act in question is reciting the Quran, and the ruling is that it is haram (prohibited) for a menstruating woman or a junub. Note that this applies to any portion of the Quran.
The prohibition includes reciting from memory or from the mus-haf.
2
Locate the Dalil
The primary proof is a hadith from Ibn Umar (ra): 'The menstruating woman and the junub should not recite anything from the Quran.' This is a sahih hadith recorded by Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud.
The hadith is explicit in its prohibition.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The imperative phrase 'la taqra'' (do not recite) indicates a prohibition. In Hanafi usul, the imperative form (nahy) generally indicates prohibition (tahrim) unless there is evidence to the contrary. Thus, the hadith directly proves the ruling.
The prohibition is clear and not open to interpretation.
4
Note the Exception for Teaching
The text provides an exception: if a woman is teaching children, she may break up the verses into individual letters or words to avoid reciting a complete ayah. This is a practical necessity (darurah).
This exception is based on scholarly consensus (ijtihad) to facilitate education.
5
Distinguish Permitted Dhikr
The menstruating woman or junub may still engage in tasbih (saying Subhanallah), tahlil (saying La ilaha illallah), and du'a. These are not Quran recitation and are not prohibited.
This highlights the difference between reciting the Quran and other forms of dhikr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۰](٦) ولا يجوز لحائض ولا لجنب قراءة القرآن
(1) عن ابن عمر عن النبي عل الله قال لا تقرء الحائض ولا الجنب شيئا من القرآن (الف) (ترمذی شریف، باب باب ما جاء في الحب والحائض لا يقرا القرآن ص ۳۲ نمبر ۱۳۱ را ابو داؤد شریف، باب فی الجنب يقرا القرآن، ص۱۳۴ نمبر ۲۲۹) علماء فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر عورت کو بچے پڑھانا ہو تو آیت کونکٹر نکڑا کر کے پڑھائے۔ البتہ تسبیح اور تہلیل پڑھ سکتی ہے، دعا پڑھ سکتی ہے تفصیل تر مندی کی حدیث ۱۳۱ کے تحت ہے۔
(6) It is not permissible for a menstruating woman or a junub to recite the Quran. (1) From Ibn Umar from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'The menstruating woman and the junub should not recite anything from the Quran.' (Tirmidhi, chapter on junub and menstruating woman not reciting Quran, p.32 no.131; Abu Dawud, chapter on junub reciting Quran, p.134 no.229). Scholars say: If a woman needs to teach children, she may teach by breaking up the verses (letter by letter). However, she can recite tasbih and tahlil, and can make du'a. Details are under Tirmidhi hadith 131.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage states the prohibition of Quran recitation for those in a state of major impurity (menstruation and janabah). The primary evidence is a hadith from Ibn Umar. The author includes a practical exception: if a woman is a teacher and cannot avoid it, she may recite the Quran piecemeal (letter by letter) to avoid complete verses. Additionally, she may still engage in dhikr like tasbih (subhanallah) and tahlil (la ilaha illallah), as well as supplications (du'a). This highlights the gravity of impurity regarding the Quran.

Key Terms
جنب (junub): One in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.
تسبیح (tasbih): Glorification of Allah, e.g., saying 'Subhanallah'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Prohibition of Touching the Quran without Wudu
Ruling that one in minor impurity cannot touch the Quran except by its cover

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on touching the Quran without wudu, cite the Quranic verse and hadith proof, and explain the exception for children.

Core Idea
Touching the Quran without wudu is haram; only a separate cover or being in a state of purity permits it.

This lesson examines the clear Hanafi ruling that a person in a state of minor impurity (muhdith) is prohibited from touching the Quran. The ruling is supported by the Quranic verse 'None touch it except the purified' and a prophetic hadith. We will explore the scope of the ruling, the distinction between attached and separate covers, and the pragmatic exception for children.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is touching the physical mushaf while in a state of minor impurity (hadath). The ruling is haram.
Recitation from memory is not prohibited.
2
Examine the primary dalil
The Quranic verse لا يمسه إلا المطهرون (al-Waqi'ah 56:79) indicates that only the purified may touch it. The exception (illa) implies prohibition for others.
Thubut is qati, dalala is zanni due to possibility of takhsis.
3
Examine the sunnah proof
The hadith in the letter to Amr ibn Hazm: 'Do not touch the Quran except when you are pure' is a direct command.
Many such hadiths are reported in Dar Qutni and Bayhaqi.
4
Consider the istihsan exception for children
Children are exempted due to difficulty in repeating wudu, their non-accountability (raf' al-qalam), and the need to encourage Quranic learning.
This istihsan overrides the general prohibition for children.
5
Apply the ruling to attached vs separate covers
An attached cover (binding) is considered part of the mushaf and requires wudu; a separate cover (ghilaf) may be touched without wudu.
This distinction is based on customary consideration (urf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۰۴] (۷) ولا يجوز للمحدث المصحف الا ان يأخذه بغلافه
حدث رانا آدمی زبانی قرآن پڑھ سکتا ہے البتہ قرآن کو چھو نہیں سکتا، اگر چھونا ہو تو کسی غلاف کے ذریعہ قرآن کو چھوئے گا۔ (1) لا يمسه الا المطهرون ( آیت ۷۹ سورۃ الواقعۃ (۵۶) (۲) حدیث میں ہے كان في كتاب النبي لعمرو بن حزم الا تمس القرآن الاعلى طهر (ب) (دار قطنی، باب فی تھی المحدث عن مس القرآن ج اول ص ۱۲۸ نمبر ۴۲۹ رسن للبیهقی ، باب الحائج لاتمس المصحف ولا تقرا القرآن، ص ۴۶۱) نمبر ۱۴۷۸) اس قسم کی بہت سی احادیث دار قطنی میں نقل کی ہیں جن سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ قرآن کو بغیر وضو
چھونا جائز نہیں ہے۔
جو غلاف اور جلد قرآن کے ساتھ چپکا ہوا ہے وہ گویا کتاب کا حصہ ہے اس لئے اس غلاف کے ساتھ چھونا جائز نہیں ہے۔ اس کے علاوہ
غلاف ہونا چاہئے۔
بچوں کو بار بار وضو کرانے میں حرج ہے (۲) وہ مرفوع القلم ہیں (۳) اس کو قرآن سے روکنے سے وہ قرآن نہیں پڑھیں گے اس لئے بچوں کو بغیر وضو کی حالت میں قرآن پڑھنے کے لئے دینا جائز ہے۔ ائنر (۱)
(7) It is not permissible for one with minor impurity (hadath) to touch the mushaf unless he takes it with its cover. A person with minor impurity can recite the Quran verbally, but cannot touch it. If he needs to touch it, he should touch it through a cover. (1) The verse: 'None touch it except the purified' (Surah al-Waqi'ah 56:79). (2) In a hadith in the letter of the Prophet to Amr ibn Hazm: 'Do not touch the Quran except when you are pure' (Dar Qutni, chapter on forbidding the muhdith from touching Quran, vol.1 p.128 no.429; Bayhaqi, chapter on menstruating woman not touching mushaf nor reciting Quran, p.461 no.1478). Many such hadiths are transmitted in Dar Qutni indicating that touching the Quran without wudu is not permissible. The cover that is attached to the Quran (binding) is considered part of the book, so touching it is not permissible without wudu. Other covers (detachable) are permitted. Regarding children: it is difficult to make them perform wudu repeatedly, they are not accountable (raf' al-qalam), and preventing them from the Quran may cause them to stop learning. Therefore, it is permissible to let children touch the Quran without wudu.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage addresses the prohibition of touching the Quran in a state of minor impurity (hadath). It distinguishes between reciting from memory (allowed) and touching the physical mushaf (not allowed). Proofs include the Quranic verse 'None touch it except the purified' and the hadith from Amr ibn Hazm. The author also clarifies that an attached cover (binding) is considered part of the mushaf, so touching it requires wudu, but a separate cover is permissible. An exception is made for children to facilitate learning, as they are not legally responsible and it would be impracticable to require wudu for them.

Key Terms
محدث (muhdith): One in a state of minor impurity (without wudu).
غلاف (ghilaf): Cover or case for the Quran, either attached (binding) or separate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is it permissible for a child without wudu to touch the Quran?
A) Yes, due to difficulty and their lack of responsibility
B) No, it is always forbidden
C) Only if they are about to read
D) Only if they have a barrier

Key Takeaway
Touching the Quran without wudu is haram; only separate covers bypass the prohibition, and children are exempted for practical reasons.

intermediate
8 min
Ruling on Purity After Menstruation Stops Within 10 Days
Condition for permissible intercourse after menstruation ends

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when intercourse becomes permissible after menstruation stops within 10 days and explain the proof from the Quran and hadith.

Core Idea
Intercourse is not permitted after menstruation stops until she performs ghusl or one full prayer time passes—because continued purity is uncertain.

This passage addresses a common situation: a woman's menstrual bleeding stops before the maximum period of 10 days. Without immediate ghusl, there is a possibility the blood may resume. Therefore, the Hanafi school requires either ghusl or the passage of one complete prayer time before intercourse becomes permissible. This ruling is based on the Quranic emphasis on thorough purification and a companion's practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sexual intercourse with a woman after her menstrual blood has stopped. The ruling is that it is not permissible (haram) until one of two conditions is met.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites Quran 2:222, specifically the word 'yattahhirna' (with tashdid), and an athar from 'Ata reporting that a woman who sees purity must still perform ghusl.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The tashdid in 'yattahhirna' indicates thorough purification, which is achieved only by ghusl. The athar confirms that ghusl is necessary before intercourse.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that intercourse is permissible as soon as the blood stops, even without ghusl, if she intends to delay ghusl. Hanafis disagree due to the possibility of blood returning.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is precautionary and based on the stronger reading of the Quranic text. It is the mufta bihi in the school.

(8) And if the blood of menstruation stops for less than ten days, then... If menstrual blood stops in less than ten days, intercourse is not permissible until she performs ghusl or a complete prayer time passes over her. Since the blood stopped in less than ten days, it is possible that the blood will return and her habit may change. Therefore, either she must perform ghusl to become completely pure—the verse 'until they become pure' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:222) if read with tashdid means 'until they become thoroughly pure,' which only happens with ghusl. This is supported by an athar: A man asked 'Ata': 'A menstruating woman sees purity but does not perform ghusl; is she permissible for her husband?' He said: 'No, until she performs ghusl.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, chapter on a man having intercourse with his wife when she sees purity).

Commentary
This passage addresses the condition for when a menstruating woman becomes permissible for intercourse after the blood stops before ten days. The key ruling: she must either perform ghusl (full ritual bath) or one complete prayer time must pass after the blood stops. The reason for caution is that if the blood stops before ten days, it may resume (as the maximum period is ten days). Therefore, she is not considered pure until ghusl or the passing of a complete prayer time (like Zuhr or Asr). The author cites the Quranic phrase 'hatta yat-harna' (until they purify themselves) with tashdid emphasizing thorough purification, which is achieved by ghusl. An athar from 'Ata' confirms that without ghusl, she is not permitted.

Key Terms
انقطع دم الحيض (inqata'a dam al-hayd): The menstrual bleeding stops completely.
الغسل (ghusl): Full ritual bath required to remove major impurity.

Key Takeaway
Intercourse with a woman whose menstrual blood stops within 10 days is not permissible until she performs ghusl or one complete prayer time passes.

Prohibition of Quran Recitation for Ha'idh and Junub
Ruling with proof and teaching exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Quran recitation for a menstruating woman and a person in major impurity, cite the primary dalil, and explain the exception for fragmented recitation in teaching.

Core Idea
Ha'idh and junub are prohibited from reciting Quran; only dhikr and fragmented teaching recitation are permitted.

This lesson examines the ruling that a menstruating woman (ha'idh) and a person in a state of major ritual impurity (junub) cannot recite the Quran. We will review the prophetic hadith that establishes this prohibition and explore an important exception: when a woman needs to teach children, she may recite the Quran in a fragmented manner (e.g., letter by letter) to avoid reciting complete verses. By the end, you will understand the evidence, the scope of the prohibition, and the practical applications for those in a state of impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The text states that it is not permissible (la yajuz) for a ha'idh or a junub to recite the Quran. Determine the hukm level: what is the ruling—haram, makruh, or other? According to the Hanafi school, this is haram because the prohibition is explicit.
The imperative 'لا تقرء' indicates a clear prohibition.
2
Locate the Dalil
The author cites the hadith from Ibn Umar: 'Do not recite anything from the Quran for the menstruating woman nor the junub.' This is a direct textual proof from the Sunnah.
The hadith is recorded in Sunan al-Tirmidhi and Sunan Abi Dawud.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The phrase 'لا تقرء' is a prohibition (nahy). In Hanafi usul, the imperative form in a prohibition indicates the act is haram unless there is evidence to the contrary. Here, no such evidence exists, so the ruling is haram.
The prohibition applies to any amount of Quranic text, not just complete verses.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf (Cross-Madhhab Difference)
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i and Maliki) allow a junub or ha'idh to recite Quran without touching the mushaf. The Hanafi school, however, holds the prohibition in all forms of recitation, based on the same hadith.
The difference arises from varying interpretations of the word 'شيئا' (anything).
5
Evaluate the Exception for Teaching
The commentary adds that if a woman needs to teach children, she may recite verses by cutting them into parts (e.g., letter by letter) to avoid reciting a complete verse. This is a concession (rukhsah) to facilitate learning.
Recitation of tasbih, tahlil, and other dhikr is always permitted.

(6) It is not permissible for a menstruating woman nor for a person in major impurity to recite the Quran.
It is not permissible for a menstruating woman and a junub to recite the Quran.
(1) From Ibn Umar from the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: "Do not recite anything from the Quran for the menstruating woman nor the junub." (Tirmidhi, Chapter on what has been narrated about the menstruating woman and the junub not reciting Quran, p. 32, no. 131; Abu Dawud, Chapter on the junub reciting Quran, p. 134, no. 229) Scholars say that if a woman needs to teach children, she should recite the verses by cutting [them into parts]. Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof. The ruling is that it is not permissible (haram) for a menstruating woman (ha'idh) or a person in a state of major ritual impurity (junub) to recite the Quran. The proof is a hadith from Ibn Umar where the Prophet explicitly prohibits recitation by these individuals. The commentary clarifies an exception: if a woman needs to teach children, she may recite verses in a fragmented manner (e.g., letter by letter) to avoid reciting a complete verse. However, she is allowed to recite tasbih (Subhanallah), tahlil (La ilaha illallah), and other supplications, as these are not considered Quran recitation. The detailed discussion is referenced to Tirmidhi hadith 131. This ruling is well-established in the Hanafi school and is based on authentic ahadith.

Key Terms
حائض (ḥā’iḍ): A woman experiencing menstrual bleeding.
جنب (junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl (e.g., after sexual intercourse or nocturnal emission).
قراءة القرآن (qirā’at al-qur’ān): Recitation of the Quran, whether aloud or silently.
مقطع (muqaṭṭa‘): Cut into parts; fragmented recitation (e.g., letter by letter) that does not constitute reciting a complete verse.

Key Takeaway

Intercourse with a Menstruating Woman When Blood Stops Early
Ruling on permissibility of intercourse when menstruation stops before 10 days.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on when intercourse becomes permissible after menstruation stops before ten days, state the two conditions that must be met, and compare this position with that of the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Intercourse is not allowed until ghusl or a full prayer time passes, due to the possibility of blood returning within the ten-day menstrual period.

This lesson examines a scenario where a woman's menstrual bleeding stops before the maximum duration of ten days. The Hanafi school rules that she is not yet considered fully pure due to the possibility of blood returning. Therefore, intercourse is prohibited until either she performs ghusl (full ritual bath) or a complete prayer time (e.g., the time for Dhuhr or Asr) has passed over her. We will explore the evidence from the Quran (2:222) and a report from Ata, and contrast the Hanafi view with that of the Shafi'i and Maliki schools.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling of the Hanafi school on intercourse when menstrual blood stops before ten days.
The school requires either ghusl or the passage of a complete prayer time.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
State which schools disagree (Shafi'i and Maliki) and their position.
They hold that ghusl is always required, regardless of timing.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the opponent's evidence from the Quran and Sunna.
They interpret 'hatta yathurna' as requiring purification with water.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Explain the Hanafi counter-argument, including the reading with tashdid and the possibility of blood returning.
The passing of a prayer time is a strong indicator of purity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference in ruling between the schools.
In Hanafi, if a prayer time passes without ghusl, intercourse is permissible; in Shafi'i, it is not.

(105) (8) If the blood of menstruation stops before ten days, then intercourse with her is not permissible until she performs ghusl or a complete prayer time passes over this menstruating woman. Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on when it becomes permissible to have intercourse with a woman after her menstruation stops. The ruling: if the bleeding stops before ten days (i.e., within the minimum period of hayd), intercourse is not permissible until one of two conditions occurs: either she performs ghusl (full ritual bath), or a complete prayer time passes over her. The reasoning is that the bleeding may resume within the ten-day period, so she is not yet considered fully pure. The Quranic evidence is from 'hatta yathurna' in Surah al-Baqarah (2:222), which the Hanafis read with tashdid (yatthaharna) to mean thorough purification through ghusl. The inter-madhhab aspect: the text later mentions that Imam Shafi'i and Imam Malik hold that ghusl is always required, regardless of the number of days. The Hanafi position is based on caution (ihtiyat) and the possibility of blood returning. The report from Ata supports the requirement of ghusl. The second condition (passing of a complete prayer time) is based on the idea that if a fard prayer becomes due on her, that indicates her purity.

Key Terms
انقطع دم الحيض (inqiṭā‘ dam al-ḥayḍ): The cessation of menstrual bleeding.
غسل (ghusl): Full ritual bath required to remove major impurity.
وقت صلوة كاملة (waqt ṣalāt kāmilah): A complete prayer time during which she could have performed a fard prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only after she performs two units of prayer.

Key Takeaway
When menstruation stops before ten days, the Hanafi school requires either ghusl or the passage of a complete prayer time before intercourse is permissible, due to the possibility of blood returning.

Intercourse with Menstruating Woman When Blood Stops at 10 Days
Ruling on intercourse when menstruation ends at exactly ten days.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits intercourse before ghusl when menstruation ends exactly at ten days, and evaluate the Shafi'i/Maliki counter-argument.

Core Idea
When menstruation completes its maximum ten days, cessation alone establishes purity, making ghusl unnecessary for intercourse.

This passage addresses a specific scenario: when a woman's menstruation ends exactly at the Hanafi maximum of ten days. The ruling here differs from other schools, and the proof turns on the interpretation of a Quranic verse and the definition of the menstrual maximum. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi distinction between the maximum period and shorter durations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and Hanafi ruling
Note that the blood stops after exactly ten days—the maximum menstrual period in the Hanafi school. The ruling permits intercourse even before ghusl.
This is the unique case where cessation alone suffices.
2
Locate the Hanafi proof
The proof is the Quranic verse 'hatta yathurna' without tashdid, interpreted by Mujahid as meaning cessation of blood. Since ten days is the maximum, no further waiting is needed.
3
State the opposing position and its proof
Imam Shafi'i and Imam Malik require ghusl in all cases, reading the verse as requiring purification with water. They cite a report in Bayhaqi.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis distinguish between cessation before ten days (where ghusl or a prayer time is needed because blood may return) and exactly at ten days (where blood cannot return). Thus, cessation alone confirms purity.
The maximum period rule is key.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, a Hanafi woman may have intercourse immediately after ten days without ghusl, whereas Shafi'i/Maliki women must always perform ghusl first.

Source Text

[۱۰۶] (۹) اور اگر حائضہ کا خون دس دن پورے ہونے پر منقطع ہو تو اس عورت سے غسل سے پہلے بھی وطی کرنا جائز ہے۔ دس دن سے زیادہ تو حیض آہی نہیں سکتا۔ اس کے بعد جو خون آئے گا وہ استحاضہ ہو گا۔ اس لئے عورت نے غسل نہیں کیا ہے تب بھی اس سے ولی کر سکتا ہے۔ البتہ بہتر یہ ہے کہ غسل کے بعد وطی کرے تاکہ مکمل یا کی پر ولی ہو۔ اس صورت میں آیت حتی بطهرن بغیر تشدید کے پر عمل ہوگا۔ جس کی تفسیر حضرت مجاہد نے فرمایا کہ جب خون منقطع ہو جائے تو وہ پاک ہوگی ۔ عبارت یہ ہے۔ عن مجاهد في قوله عزوجل (ولا تقربوهن حتى يطهرن) حتى ينقطع الدم فاذا تطهرن قال يقول اذا اغتسلن (سن لليبقى ، باب الحائض لا تو طا حتى تظهر و تغتسل ، ج اول ص ۴۶۲ نمبر ۱۳۸۲) امام شافعی اور امام مالک کے نزدیک ہر حال میں غسل ہے۔ پہلے ولی کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ ان کے نزدیک حتی بطهرن کا ترجمہ طہارت بالماء ہے۔ اور اثر بیھقی سے استدلال کرتے ہیں کہ مکمل طہارت ہونی چاہئے تب وطی کرے۔
(9) And if her blood stops for ten days, it is permissible to have intercourse with her before ghusl.
(106) (9) And if the menstruating woman's blood stops after completing ten days, then it is permissible to have intercourse with this woman even before ghusl. Menstruation cannot exceed ten days. Blood that comes after that will be istihadah. Therefore, even if the woman has not performed ghusl, her husband can have intercourse with her. However, it is better to have intercourse after ghusl so that it is done with complete purity. In this case, the verse 'hatta yathurna' without tashdid is acted upon. Its interpretation was given by Mujahid: When the blood stops, she is pure. The expression is: From Mujahid regarding the saying of Allah Almighty 'Wa la taqrabuhunna hatta yathurna' - until the blood stops. Then 'fa idha tatahharna' he says: when they perform ghusl. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Chapter on the menstruating woman not being approached until she becomes pure and performs ghusl, vol. 1, p. 462, no. 1382) According to Imam Shafi'i and Imam Malik, ghusl is always required. Intercourse before ghusl is not permissible. According to them, 'hatta yathurna' means purification with water. They argue using the Bayhaqi report that complete purification must be done before intercourse.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling for the situation where menstruation completes exactly ten days (the maximum duration). In this case, it is permissible to have intercourse even before she performs ghusl. The reason: since ten days is the maximum period for hayd, any blood after that is istihadah (non-menstrual bleeding), so she is considered pure once the bleeding stops. The preferred action is still to perform ghusl first, but it is not legally required for permissibility. The Quranic evidence is the verse 'hatta yathurna' without tashdid, interpreted by Mujahid as meaning the cessation of blood. The inter-madhhab dispute: Imam Shafi'i and Imam Malik require ghusl in all cases, reading the verse as requiring purification with water. The Hanafi school allows intercourse without ghusl in this specific case, relying on the interpretation of Mujahid and the principle that the maximum period has been completed.

Key Terms
عشرة أيام (‘asharat ayyām): The maximum duration of menstruation (hayd) in the Hanafi school.
استحاضة (istiḥāḍah): Non-menstrual vaginal bleeding that occurs outside the normal menstrual period.
بعد الغسل (ba‘d al-ghusl): After performing the ritual bath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, ghusl is required in all cases.
B) No, ghusl is not required, but it is better to do it.
C) Yes, but only if she wants to pray afterwards.
D) No, and ghusl is not even recommended.

Key Takeaway
When menstruation completes its maximum (ten days), the mere cessation of blood is sufficient to permit intercourse, making ghusl recommended but not required.

Purity Between Blood Flows is Part of Hayd
Ruling on purity between blood flows within hayd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on days without bleeding within the menstrual period, identify the qiyas used as proof, and apply the ruling to a scenario.

Core Idea
Days without blood within the menstrual period are legally treated as menstruation — continuity is not required.

Menstrual bleeding is often intermittent — a woman may see blood for a day, then a gap, then blood again. The Hanafi school rules that any day falling within the ten-day maximum period of hayd is considered menstruation, even if no blood appeared. This lesson explains the ruling and its proof by analogy with the nisab of zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the day(s) of purity (no blood) occurring between two blood flows within the maximum ten-day period. The ruling is that these days are considered part of hayd (menstruation), with all its legal consequences.
This applies only within the ten-day maximum; beyond that, the rulings change.
2
Locate the dalil
The text does not cite a direct Quranic or hadith proof but uses qiyas (analogy) with the nisab for zakat. The asl (original case) is zakat nisab: it is sufficient that the nisab exists at the beginning and end of the lunar year; possession in between is not required.
The far' (new case) is the days of purity within the menstrual period.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (illa)
The effective cause (illa) is that in both cases, continuity of a condition is not required; the presence of the condition at the extremes suffices to establish the ruling for the whole interval.
The illa is jaliyyah (clear), extracted by tanqih al-manat.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools (notably the Shafi'is) may require continuous bleeding for the entire period to be counted as hayd, or they may treat the gap as purity (tuhr).
The Hanafi position is mukhtalaf fih, meaning there is legitimate disagreement.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on this qiyas and the common experience that menstrual blood is often intermittent. The mufta bihi is that days of purity within the ten-day period are part of hayd.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text

[۱۰۷] (۱۰) وہ پا کی جو دوخون کے درمیان ہو حیض کی مدت میں تو وہ جاری خون کی طرح ہے۔ ات عموما ایسا ہوتا ہے کہ کچھ دیر خون آتا ہے پھر بند ہو جاتا ہے، پھر آتا ہے پھر بند ہو جاتا ہے ، حیض کا خون مسلسل نہیں آتا رہتا ہے۔ اس لئے حیض کی مدت کے درمیان پاکی اور طہر ہو تو اس کا حکم بھی خون آنے ہی کی طرح ہے۔ یعنی اس مدت میں عورت نماز نہیں پڑھے گی اور نہ اس کا شوہر اس سے وطی کرے گا۔ مثلا پہلے دن خون آیا پھر خون بند رہا پھر دسویں دن خون آیا تو پہلے دن سے لیکر دس دن تک حیض ہی شمار کیا جائے گا اور اس کا حکم حیض ہی کی طرح ہوگا ۔ جس طرح نصاب زکوۃ میں شروع سال اور اخیر سال میں نصاب پورا ہو جانا کافی ہے اسی طرح حیض کے شروع دن میں اور اخیر دن میں خون آجائے تو تمان دن حیض ہی شمار کر دیا جائے گا۔ چاہے درمیان میں خون نہ آیا ہو (۲) عموما ہمیشہ خون آتا بھی نہیں ہے ۔ اس لئے مسلسل خون آنے کی شرط نہیں لگائی گئی۔
(10) The purity that occurs between two blood flows within the menstrual period is like flowing blood.
(107) (10) The purity that occurs between two blood flows within the menstrual period is like flowing blood. Generally, it happens that blood comes for a while, then stops, then comes again, then stops again. Menstrual blood does not flow continuously. Therefore, if there is purity and cleanliness during the menstrual period, its ruling is like that of the blood flow. That is, in that period, the woman will not pray, nor will her husband have intercourse with her. For example, if blood came on the first day, then stopped, then came on the tenth day, then from the first day to the tenth day it will be counted as menstruation and its ruling will be like that of menstruation. Just as in the nisab of zakat, it is sufficient that the nisab is complete at the beginning and end of the year, similarly, if blood comes at the beginning and at the end of the menstrual period, all days in between will be counted as menstruation, even if no blood came in between. (2) Generally, blood does not always flow continuously, so the condition of continuous bleeding is not imposed.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling about the status of days in which there is no bleeding but that fall within the overall menstrual period. The ruling is that such days of purity are considered as part of the menstrual period; i.e., they are treated as if blood were flowing. The woman must not pray and intercourse is prohibited during these days as well. The proof is based on the common pattern that menstrual bleeding is often interrupted. The author draws an analogy (qiyas) with the calculation of nisab for zakat: just as in zakat, it suffices that the nisab is present at the beginning and end of the year, similarly for hayd, if blood is seen at the start and end of the ten-day period (i.e., on the first and tenth day), the entire interval is considered menstruation, regardless of whether blood flowed continuously. This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school and emphasizes that continuous flow is not a condition for counting days as hayd.

Key Terms
طهر متخلل (ṭuhr mutakhalil): Purity (absence of blood) occurring between two blood flows within the menstrual period.
جاری خون (jārī dam): Flowing blood, i.e., continuous bleeding.
نصاب زکوۃ (niṣāb zakāt): The minimum amount of wealth (e.g., gold or silver) that makes zakat obligatory.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, interrupted bleeding within the ten-day period is all considered hayd, based on qiyas with zakat nisab.

Intercourse Before Ghusl After Hayd: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i/Maliki
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute on intercourse before ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intercourse before ghusl after hayd and explain the Quranic proof and opposition from Shafi'i/Maliki.

Core Idea
Cessation of blood, not ghusl, removes the prohibition of intercourse in hayd.

This lesson explores a key inter-madhhab difference: when exactly is intercourse permitted after a woman's menstrual period ends? The Hanafi school holds that once the blood stops at the maximum duration of ten days, intercourse is permissible even before ghusl. We will examine the Quranic verse used by both sides, the interpretation of Mujahid, and the practical consequence of this dispute.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: intercourse is permissible immediately when blood stops at ten days, even without ghusl.
The text says 'jaza wati'uha qabl al-ghusl' – it is permissible.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
Analyze the Quranic verse (2:222) 'hatta yat-hurna' interpreted by Mujahid as cessation of blood. Note that the word is without tashdid.
Hanafi: 'yathurna' means the blood stops; ghusl is recommended but not required for permissibility.
3
Identify the opposing view
State that Shafi'i and Maliki require ghusl before intercourse. They interpret 'yat-hurna' as purification with water and cite a different athar.
Their dalil: complete purification (ghusl) is necessary.
4
Compare the interpretations
Contrast the two readings of the same verse. Note the difference in tashdid and the influence of the athar from Bayhaqi.
Hanafi responds: the verse without tashdid indicates mere cessation; the athar is not strong enough to obligate ghusl.
5
Determine the thamarah (practical consequence)
The ruling difference: Hanafi allows immediate intercourse; Shafi'i/Maliki require waiting until after ghusl.
This affects timing and obligation to perform ghusl before intimacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if her blood stops at ten days, it is permissible to have intercourse with her before ghusl. [106] (9) And if the menstruating woman's blood stops upon completion of ten days, then intercourse with this woman is permissible even before ghusl. Because menstruation cannot exceed ten days. Whatever blood comes after that is istihadah. Therefore, even if she has not performed ghusl, her husband can have intercourse with her. However, it is better to have intercourse after ghusl so that the intercourse is in complete purity. In this case, the verse 'until they are purified' (without tashdid) is acted upon. Its interpretation by Mujahid is: when the blood stops, she is pure. The text: From Mujahid regarding the saying of Allah 'Do not approach them until they are purified': until the blood stops, and when they purify themselves, he said: when they perform ghusl. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi). Imam Shafi'i and Imam Malik hold that ghusl is required in all cases; intercourse before ghusl is not permissible. According to them, 'until they are purified' means purification with water, and they cite the athar of Bayhaqi that complete purification must occur before intercourse.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling with its proof and the opposing view. The ruling states that when a woman's menstrual blood stops exactly on the tenth day, intercourse becomes permissible even if she has not yet performed ghusl. The Hanafi position relies on the interpretation of the Quranic verse 'hatta yat-hurna' (without tashdid) as meaning cessation of blood, supported by the interpretation of Mujahid. The hadith from Bayhaqi is used to show that when the blood stops, she is considered pure. The Shafi'i and Maliki schools, however, require ghusl before intercourse, interpreting the verse as purification with water and citing a different athar. The Hanafi school's reasoning is based on the maximum duration of hayd being ten days; after that, any blood is istihadah, not hayd, so the prohibition is lifted. The commentary clarifies that while intercourse is permissible before ghusl, it is better to perform ghusl first for completeness.

Key Terms

وطی (wati): Sexual intercourse between husband and wife.
تشديد (tashdid): The doubling of a consonant in Arabic script; here it refers to the shaddah on the letter 'ta' in 'yat-hurna' or 'yat-tahharna'.

Intermittent Purity Within Hayd is Like Flowing Blood
Ruling on intermittent purity within hayd period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intermittent purity within the hayd period and apply it to a scenario by identifying the condition that the interval does not exceed ten days.

Core Idea
Purity between two bleedings within ten days is still hayd – continuous flow is not required.

Many women experience menstrual bleeding that is not continuous; it may come and go over several days. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position that a period of purity (no blood) between two appearances of blood within the maximum hayd duration (ten days) is legally considered part of hayd. Therefore, the woman must abstain from prayer and marital relations during the entire interval, just as if blood were flowing continuously.

Guided Path
1
Identify key terms
Focus on the words 'tahr' (purity) and 'takhallala' (intervening). The purity is the clean period, and takhallal describes that it occurs between two bleedings.
This is the core concept: an intervening clean period.
2
Understand the analogy
The passage says the purity is 'like continuous blood' (ka al-dam al-jari). This means the legal rulings of hayd apply to the clean days as if blood were present.
The analogy contrasts with the idea that only days with actual blood count.
3
Identify the condition
The entire span from the first appearance of blood to the last must be within the maximum hayd period of ten days. If it exceeds ten days, the extra days become istihadah.
The passage uses the zakat nisab analogy: the beginning and end matter, not the middle.
4
Apply the ruling
During the entire interval, the woman abstains from prayer, fasting, and intercourse. She does not make up prayers for those days.
The clean days are not considered true purity (tuhr) for the purpose of prayer.
5
Distinguish from istihadah
If the bleeding continues beyond ten days, the first ten days are hayd, and the remainder is istihadah (non-menstrual bleeding). The clean interval rule applies only within the ten-day window.
This prevents confusion when cycles are irregular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the purity that occurs between two bleedings within the period of hayd is like continuous blood. [107] (10) That purity which is between two bleedings within the duration of hayd is like flowing blood. It is common that blood comes for a while, then stops, then comes again, then stops; menstrual blood does not flow continuously. Therefore, if there is purity and cleanliness during the hayd period, its ruling is the same as that of blood coming. That is, during this period, the woman will not pray, nor will her husband have intercourse with her. For example, if blood comes on the first day, then stops, then comes on the tenth day, then from the first to the tenth day will be counted as hayd, and its ruling will be the same as hayd. Commentary
This passage explains a key rule about menstruation: intermittent bleeding or spotting within the maximum hayd period (10 days) is still considered part of hayd. The purity between two bleedings is treated as if blood were flowing continuously. The author uses the analogy of zakat nisab: just as the nisab only needs to be present at the beginning and end of the lunar year, similarly, if blood appears on the first day and again on the tenth day, the entire period is hayd. The practical consequence is that the woman must abstain from prayer and her husband from intercourse during the entire interval, even on days when there is no blood. This ruling is based on the juristic principle of continuity (istimrariyah) and avoids difficulty for women.

Key Terms
طهر (tahr): A period of purity (no blood) that occurs within the hayd period.
تخلل (takhallala): To intervene or occur between two things; here, a period of purity between two bleedings.

Key Takeaway

Minimum and Maximum Duration of Purity (Tuhr)
Ruling on minimum purity interval with evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi minimum purity period and explain its evidence from narration and analogy.

Core Idea
Minimum purity between two hayd periods: 15 days (no maximum).

This lesson establishes a foundational rule in menstrual jurisprudence: the minimum interval of purity (tuhr) between two hayd cycles is fifteen days. There is no upper limit; a woman may go years without menstruating. This rule is essential for distinguishing between true menstruation (hayd) and non-menstrual bleeding (istihadah). The ruling is based on a narration from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and an analogy with the minimum residency period for a traveler.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Identify the two-part rule: (1) The minimum duration of purity between two menstrual periods is fifteen days. (2) There is no maximum limit; purity can extend indefinitely.
This is a wajib ruling because it is a necessary condition for correctly applying hayd and istihadah rules.
2
Trace the Evidence
The author provides two pieces of evidence: (a) a narration from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that the minimum purity is fifteen days, and (b) an analogy (qiyas) with the ruling that a traveler must intend to stay at least fifteen days to be considered a resident (and thus shorten prayers).
The author notes that he could not verify the exact wording of the narration, indicating a degree of caution, but the analogy reinforces the ruling.
3
Apply the Consequence
Understand the practical impact: if a woman experiences bleeding fewer than fifteen days after the end of the previous hayd, that bleeding is not a new hayd but istihadah (non-menstrual bleeding). This affects rules of prayer, fasting, and marital relations.
The fifteen-day rule is a fixed threshold; any shorter interval is legally considered continuous impurity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واقل الطهر خمسة عشر يوما ولا غاية لاكثره [۱۰۸] (۱۱) دو حیضوں کے درمیان کم سے کم طہر پندرہ دن ہونگے اور کوئی حد نہیں ہے زیادہ طہر کے لئے۔ دو حیضوں کے درمیان کم سے کم پندرہ دن ظہر ہو گا اور زیادہ طہر کے لئے کوئی حد نہیں ہے۔ دو سال اور تین سال بھی عورت کو حیض نہیں آتا ہے۔ (۱) ابراہیم نخعی سے منقول ہے کہ کم سے کم طہر کی مدت پندرہ دن ہوتی ہے۔ اس لئے پندرہ دن ہونگے ۔ یہ قول مجھے نہیں ملا اور نہ صاحب درایہ کو ملا ہے (۲) اقامت کی مدت پندرہ دن ہے اس سے استدلال کیا جا سکتا ہے کہ طہر کی مدت پندرہ دن ہو۔
And the minimum of purity is fifteen days, and there is no limit to its maximum. [108] (11) Between two menstruations, the minimum purity will be fifteen days, and there is no limit for more purity. Between two menstruations, the minimum purity will be fifteen days, and there is no limit for more purity. A woman may not menstruate for two or three years. (1) It is narrated from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that the minimum period of purity is fifteen days. Therefore, it is fifteen days. I have not found this saying, nor did Sahib al-Diraya find it. (2) The period of residence (for a traveler) is fifteen days; this can be used as evidence that the purity period is fifteen days.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This lesson establishes a fixed rule: the minimum interval of purity (tuhr) between two menstrual periods is fifteen days, based on transmitted reports and analogy. There is no maximum; a woman may go years without menstruating. The author mentions two bases: (1) a narration from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i setting fifteen days as the minimum, though the author notes he could not verify this specific report; (2) an analogy with the ruling on the minimum period of residency for a traveler (also fifteen days in the Hanafi school). This rule is critical for distinguishing between hayd and istihadah: if bleeding occurs before fifteen days of purity, it is istihadah, not a new hayd.

Key Terms
طهر (tahr): The interval of purity between two menstrual periods.
لا غاية لاكثره (la ghayata li aktharihi): There is no fixed upper limit; it can be indefinitely long.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 15 days
C) 1 month
D) No minimum

Key Takeaway
The minimum pure interval between two menstrual periods is fifteen days; this rule is based on transmitted authority and analogy.

Definition of Istihadah Blood by Duration
Definition of istihadah by duration with cross-madhhab comparison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of istihadah, apply it to a given bleeding duration, and identify when the color criterion is used as an alternative.

Core Idea
Istihadah is any bleeding outside 3–10 days; the woman is legally pure and must worship.

This lesson defines istihadah (irregular bleeding) by its duration—less than three full days or more than ten. While the Hanafi school relies on duration as the primary criterion, the passage also introduces a color-based approach from Imam Ahmad. Understanding this definition is essential for distinguishing between menstrual blood (hayd) and chronic bleeding, which affects a woman's worship and marital relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi definition
Read the opening sentence: istihadah is blood seen for less than 3 days or more than 10 days. This is the core definition you must memorize.
The minimum hayd is 3 days, maximum 10 days. Anything outside is istihadah.
2
Apply the duration boundaries
Take the example given: 2 days of bleeding followed by 11 dry days → the 2 days are istihadah. Similarly, bleeding beyond 10 days is istihadah.
If bleeding lasts exactly 3 or 10 days, it is hayd (provided other conditions).
3
Contrast with the color-based criterion
Note that Imam Ahmad uses blood color: black/red = hayd, yellow/pale = istihadah. The Hanafi school prefers duration, but for a perplexed woman (mutahayyirah) with no habit, color may be used as a fallback.
The hadith of Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh is cited as evidence for color, but Hanafis interpret it for the woman who cannot determine duration.
4
Extract the legal effect of istihadah
A woman in istihadah is legally pure: she prays, fasts, and can have intercourse. The blood does not break wudu after each act, but she must perform wudu for each prayer time.
This is similar to a chronic nosebleed (ru'af mustamirr).
5
Memorize the key terms
Know 'istihadah' and 'mutahayyirah' – they appear in fiqh texts and fatwas. The definition is a memorization anchor for this topic.

And the blood of istihadah is what a woman sees for less than three days or more than ten days. [10](13) [109] (12) And the blood of istihadah is that which a woman sees for less than three days or more than ten days. According to the Hanafis, a woman will have menstrual blood for a minimum of three days. But for example, if blood comes for two days and then no blood for eleven days, then that two-day blood will be counted as istihadah. Similarly, if blood comes for more than ten days, it will be counted as istihadah. For those imams (like Imam Ahmad) who base the determination of hayd on the color of blood (black or very red), the issue of istihadah is very simple: when black or extremely red blood comes, they consider it hayd, and when yellow, pale, or muddy blood comes, they consider it istihadah. And in the state of istihadah, the woman will pray, fast, and her husband can have intercourse with her. The scholars say that for a perplexed woman (mutahayyirah) who has no habitual pattern, it is better to decide between hayd and istihadah based on color. Because there is a hadith from Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh: she was experiencing istihadah, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to her: 'When the blood of hayd comes, it is black blood that is known; when that happens, abstain from prayer; when the other (blood) comes, perform wudu and pray.' (Abu Dawud). Thus, Imam Ahmad decided based on color of blood.

Commentary
This lesson defines istihadah (irregular bleeding) by its duration: less than three days or more than ten days. The Hanafi school sets the minimum for hayd at three days and the maximum at ten days. Therefore, any bleeding outside this range is istihadah. The passage also discusses an alternative criterion based on blood color, attributed to Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, where the quality of blood (black/red vs. pale/yellow) determines hayd vs. istihadah. For a woman who has no consistent pattern (mutahayyirah), the color criterion is recommended. However, the Hanafi school's primary criterion is duration, not color. The hadith of Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh is cited to show that the Prophet distinguished by color, but Hanafis prioritize the duration-based definition as more objective. The ruling for istihadah is that the woman is considered legally pure for worship: she prays, fasts, and can have intercourse.

Key Terms
استحاضة (istihadah): Irregular vaginal bleeding outside the normal hayd duration (less than 3 days or more than 10 days).
متحیرة (mutahayyirah): A woman who has no regular habit (adat) of menstruation and cannot determine her hayd duration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Istihadah is defined by duration in the Hanafi school: bleeding under 3 days or over 10 days; the woman remains legally pure and must observe all acts of worship.

Istihadah: Ruled Like a Nosebleed – Worship Continues
Ruling with proof: istihadah like nosebleed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istihadah and explain its dalil (proof) from the hadith of Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh.

Core Idea
Istihadah is like a nosebleed – it does not break wudu nor prevent prayer, fasting, or marital relations.

Many assume any vaginal bleeding invalidates worship. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position: istihadah (non-menstrual chronic bleeding) is treated like a chronic nosebleed. The Prophet ﷺ explicitly distinguished it from hayd. You will learn the ruling and the proof from the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Istihadah is chronic irregular bleeding. The Hanafi ruling is that it does not invalidate prayer, fasting, or intercourse. It is permissible to continue all acts of worship.
The ruling is based on the analogy with nosebleed, which is a chronic condition that does not break wudu.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith of Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh narrated by Aisha in Sahih al-Bukhari. The Prophet ﷺ said: 'That is only a vein, not menstruation.'
The hadith directly addresses the confusion between hayd and istihadah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The key phrase 'innama dhalika ‘irq' (that is only a vein) indicates that the bleeding is from a vein, not from the menstrual source. Therefore, the legal impurity of hayd does not apply.
This justifies the ruling that istihadah does not prevent worship.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Early Hanafi scholars differed on whether istihadah breaks wudu. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that it does not nullify wudu, but the woman must perform wudu for each prayer time if bleeding continues.
The hadith is the primary authority; the school follows it.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is that a woman with istihadah prays and fasts normally, may have intercourse, and only needs to renew wudu at each prayer time (not for each bleeding).
This avoids the hardship of abandoning worship for prolonged periods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[10] (13) The ruling of istihadah is like the ruling of a nosebleed. It does not prevent prayer, nor fasting, nor intercourse. In the state of istihadah, all these are permissible. There is evidence for this in the hadith: from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh said to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): 'O Messenger of Allah, I am not pure; should I abandon prayer?' The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'That is only a vein, not menstruation. When the hayd comes, leave prayer; when it goes, wash the blood from yourself and pray.' (Bukhari, chapter)

Commentary
This lesson delivers a crucial ruling: istihadah (chronic irregular bleeding) does not invalidate acts of worship or prevent marital relations. The analogy is to a nosebleed (ru'af), which is a chronic condition that does not break wudu according to the stronger Hanafi view (though there is detail). The proof is the well-known hadith of Fatima bint Abi Hubaysh, where the Prophet explicitly distinguishes between hayd (menstruation) and istihadah (bleeding from a vein). He instructs her to pray during istihadah after washing off the blood. Thus, a woman experiencing istihadah must continue praying, fasting, and may have intercourse with her husband. The hadith establishes that istihadah is not a legal impurity that prevents worship.

Key Terms
استحاضة (istihadah): Irregular uterine bleeding not related to menstruation.
عرق (irq): A vein (from which the blood of istihadah flows).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Prophet ﷺ made it clear: istihadah is not hayd; women must continue their worship, performing wudu for each prayer time.

The Ruling for a Mustahadah Without Prior Habit
Ruling with proof and inter/intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a mustahadah with no prior menstrual habit and explain the dalil that supports it.

Core Idea
A woman with no menstrual habit counts ten days as hayd each month; the remaining days are istihadah.

The Hanafi school provides a clear rule for a woman who begins menstruating but experiences continuous bleeding, making it impossible to establish a habit. This lesson examines that ruling, its proof from hadith, and the differences between the Hanafi view and that of Imam al-Shafi'i, as well as an internal Hanafi variation from Imam Abu Yusuf. Understanding this helps you see how Hanafi fiqh handles uncertainty through fixed maximums.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Subject
The subject is a woman who reaches puberty and immediately experiences continuous bleeding (mustahadah) with no prior menstrual habit.
No habit means no set pattern of days or blood color to refer to.
2
State the Ruling
The Hanafi ruling: ten days of each month are counted as hayd (menses); the remaining days are istihadah (irregular bleeding). She prays and fasts on the istihadah days.
This is based on the maximum limit of hayd being ten days.
3
Examine the Proof
The proof is the hadith: 'The minimum of menses is three days, and its maximum is ten days' (Darqutni). This establishes ten days as the maximum period for hayd.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted by the Hanafis.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Difference
Imam Abu Yusuf differentiates: for prayer and fasting, only three days are hayd (the minimum), but for guardianship and iddah, ten days count. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that all ten days are hayd for all purposes.
This difference arises from caution versus textual adherence.
5
Compare with the Shafi'i View
Imam al-Shafi'i determines hayd by blood color: black or red blood is hayd, other colors are istihadah. The Hanafis argue that the color-based hadith applies when a habit exists; without a habit, the maximum duration rule prevails.
The practical consequence: Hanafi counts fixed ten days; Shafi'i examines color daily.

Source Text

And if she begins (menstruating) at puberty as a mustahadah, then her menses are ten days each month, and the remainder is istihadah. And she fasts and prays. (A) (Tirmidhi, Chapter on What came about that the mustahadah makes wudu for every prayer, p.33 no.126). From this hadith it is known that if a woman has a known habit of menses and blood comes for more than ten days, then whatever is beyond the habit is all istihadah blood. (112) (15) If after reaching puberty she became mustahadah from the beginning, then her menses are ten days each month, and the remainder is istihadah. A woman gets her first blood and it exceeds ten days, and she becomes mustahadah, and no habit could be established to which to refer, and blood comes all the time, then for such a woman ten days per month are counted as menses, and the remaining days are istihadah. (1) Each month three days are certainly menses; the remaining seven days are doubtful. However, according to the Hanafis, the maximum of menses is ten days, so they count ten days as menses. The maximum of menses is ten days; its proof in issue number one the hadith passed: 'The minimum of menses is three days, and its maximum is ten days' (Darqutni no.836). Benefit: Imam Abu Yusuf's opinion is that regarding prayer and fasting, three days are menses, and the remaining days she will perform prayers and fasts, and regarding guardianship, six days are counted as menses so that she does not marry for ten days. Their proof is ahadith in which black and red blood are called menses. This hadith passed in issue no.12 with reference to Abu Dawud. The words of the hadith were: 'Indeed it is black blood known' (Abu Dawud no.304).

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school for a woman who becomes mustahadah (experiencing chronic irregular bleeding) from the onset of puberty without a prior menstrual habit. The core ruling is that her hayd (menses) is fixed at ten days per month, and the remaining days are istihadah (non-menstrual bleeding). This is based on the hadith from Darqutni setting the maximum hayd at ten days. The text then provides a benefit: Imam Abu Yusuf distinguishes between prayer/fasting (where only three days are hayd) and guardianship/iddah (where ten days count), but the general Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa) counts all ten days as hayd for all purposes. The passage also records the Shafi'i position, which relies on blood color (black/red indicates hayd) rather than a fixed duration. The Hanafi school prioritizes the ten-day maximum hadith over the color-based hadith because the latter is contextual. The commentary emphasizes that for a woman with no habit, the ruling is straightforward: ten days hayd, rest istihadah.

Key Terms
مستحاضة (mustahādah): A woman who experiences irregular bleeding beyond the normal menstrual period, i.e., chronic istihadah.
حيض (ḥayḍ): The menstrual blood itself or the menstrual period; here referring to the ten days counted as menses.
استحاضة (istiḥāḍah): Irregular bleeding that is not part of the menstrual cycle; here the remainder of the month after the ten hayd days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Definition of Nifas and Ruling on Blood Before Childbirth
Definition and ruling on prenatal bleeding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define nifas and state the Hanafi ruling on blood seen before childbirth, distinguishing it from hayd.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, any blood seen during pregnancy before childbirth is istihadah, not nifas or hayd.

This lesson clarifies a crucial distinction for women's fiqh: the definition of postnatal bleeding (nifas) and the ruling on prenatal bleeding. Understanding this ensures correct practice of prayer and fasting during pregnancy.

Guided Path
1
Define nifas linguistically and legally
Identify the Arabic term 'nifas' and its derivation from 'nafs'. Note that it refers specifically to blood after childbirth.
The term 'aqib' in the text means 'after'.
2
Identify the ruling on blood before childbirth
The text states clearly that any blood seen by a pregnant woman, or during labor before the child exits, is istihadah.
This ruling is based on the definition of nifas and the principle that a pregnant woman does not menstruate.
3
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Consider a pregnant woman who sees spotting before delivery. According to the Hanafi ruling, she is not in nifas or hayd, so she must continue praying and fasting, performing wudu for each prayer.
This differs from other schools that may have different views.

[۱۱۴] (۱۷) والنفاس هو الدم الخارج عقيب الولادة۔ نفاس کا بیان نفاس سے مشتق ہے۔ یعنی وہ خون جو نفس یعنی انسان نکلنے کی وجہ سے نکلے۔ انت عقیب : بعد میں، پیچھے [۱۱۵) (۱۸) والدم الذي تراه الحامل او تراه في حال الولادة قبل خروج الولد فهو استحاضة۔ حاملہ عورت حمل کی حالت میں خون دیکھے یا بچہ پیدا ہونے سے پہلے عورت کو جو خون آتا ہے وہ استحاضہ کا خون ہے۔ (۱) کیونکہ نفاس اس خون کو کہتے ہیں جو بچہ پیدا ہونے کے بعد ہو اور یہ بچہ پیدا ہونے سے پہلے ہے۔ اور حیض اس لئے نہیں ہو سکتا کہ وہ (ب) آپ نے فرمایا مستحاضہ کے سلسلے میں کہ وہ حیض کے زمانے میں نماز چھوڑ دے گی جس میں حیض آیا کرتا تھا۔ پھر غسل کرے گی اور ہر نماز کے وقت وضو کرے گی اور روز ور کھے گی اور نماز پڑھے گی (ب) ہر نماز کے لئے وضو کرو اگر چہ خون چٹائی پر ٹپکتا ر ہے۔
[114] (17) And nifas is the blood that comes out after childbirth. Explanation of nifas: Derived from 'nafs' (soul/person), i.e., the blood that comes out due to the exit of the human being. 'Aqib' means after, behind. [115] (18) And the blood that a pregnant woman sees or sees during the state of childbirth before the exit of the child is istihadah. If a pregnant woman sees blood during pregnancy or the blood that comes to a woman before the birth of the child, it is blood of istihadah. (1) Because nifas is called the blood that comes after the birth of the child, and this is before the birth of the child. And it cannot be hayd because... (B) He (the Prophet) said regarding the mustahadah that she will leave prayer during the days of her menses which she used to menstruate, then she will bathe and perform wudu at every prayer time, and she will fast and pray. (B) Perform wudu for every prayer, even if blood drips on the mat.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines nifas (postnatal bleeding) and clarifies the ruling for blood that appears before childbirth. Nifas is lexically derived from 'nafs' (soul/person) and refers to the blood emitted after the expulsion of the child (i.e., after birth). The passage then clarifies that any blood seen by a pregnant woman before the birth of the child is not nifas and not hayd; it is istihadah. The reasoning given is that nifas is specifically post-birth, and it cannot be hayd because the woman is pregnant (hayd does not occur during pregnancy according to the Hanafi school). The brief cross-reference to the mustahadah hadith reinforces that blood during pregnancy is treated as istihadah, requiring wudu for each prayer time. This lesson establishes the precise legal definition of nifas and the permissibility of prayer and fasting during pregnancy even if bleeding occurs.

Key Terms
نفاس (nifās): Postnatal bleeding; blood that comes out after the birth of a child (after delivery).
حامل (ḥāmil): Pregnant woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on blood seen by a woman during pregnancy before childbirth?
A) It is nifas
B) It is hayd
C) It is istihadah
D) It requires ghusl

Key Takeaway
Blood seen during pregnancy before childbirth is istihadah, requiring only wudu for prayer, not ghusl or cessation of worship.

Ruling on Mustahadah's Wudu and its Proof
Ruling with hadith proof for mustahadah's wudu validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a mustahadah's wudu and explain the interpretation of the hadith proof that 'at every prayer' means 'at every prayer time'.

Core Idea
A mustahadah's wudu lasts for the entire prayer time, not just the prayer itself.

This lesson addresses a common concern: how can a woman with chronic bleeding maintain purity for prayer? The Shari'ah provides ease by allowing her to perform wudu once per prayer time. We will examine the hadith proof and the Hanafi legal reasoning that interprets 'at every prayer' as 'at every prayer time'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the wudu of a mustahadah. What is the ruling? The mustahadah must perform wudu for each prayer time, and that wudu remains valid until the time exits.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) instructing the mustahadah to perform wudu for every prayer. Identify the source and its transmission status.
Hadith from Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah — ahad hadith, zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'tat·washsha' (perform wudu) indicates obligation in Hanafi usul. However, what does 'at every prayer' mean? The Hanafi school interprets it idiomatically as 'at every prayer time'.
Arabic usage: 'Come at zuhr' means at zuhr time, not the prayer itself.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Zufar holds that wudu breaks before the next prayer time enters. Imam al-Shafi'i also requires wudu for each prayer, but the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions are close in practice.
Shafi'i: one wudu covers fard and nafl within that prayer's time.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that wudu remains valid for the entire time. Imam Zufar's view is marjuh. The majority Hanafi view aligns with the hadith's apparent meaning and convenience for the mustahadah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة

امام شافعی کے نزدیک احادیث کی بنا پر ہر نماز کے لئے وضو کیا جائے گا اور اس کے تحت میں نوافل پڑھ سکتے ہیں احادیث میں ہر نماز کے لئے غسل کرنے کا حکم ہے وہ استباب کے طور پر ہے یا علاج کے طور پر ہے سلسل البول : جن کو ہر وقت پیشاب کا قطرہ آتا رہتا ہو، الرعاف الدائم : ہمیشہ نکسیر پھوٹتی رہتی ہو، لا یرقاً : خون بند نہ ہوتا ہو
امام زفر کے نزدیک فرض نماز کا وقت داخل ہونے سے پہلے وضو ٹوٹے گا۔
The Book of Purity. Obligations and supererogatory (prayers). When the time exits, their wudu becomes invalid, and they must renew wudu for another prayer. [...] And nifas is blood that emerges after childbirth. [...] The hadith: The Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) said concerning the mustahadah: 'She leaves prayer during the days of her menses, then she bathes and performs wudu for every prayer, and fasts and prays.' [...] 'Perform wudu for every prayer, even if blood drips on the mat.' [...] From these hadiths it is understood that she will perform wudu for every prayer. However, in our madhhab, the excused person performs wudu for the time of prayer, not for the prayer itself, because idiomatically 'prayer' is used to mean the time of prayer. So 'at every prayer' means 'at every prayer time'. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, one can also pray many supererogatory prayers within one wudu. Thus the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions are close. [...] According to Imam al-Shafi'i, based on hadiths, wudu is for each prayer and nafl can be prayed within it. The command for ghusl for every prayer is either mustahabb (recommended) or as a cure. [...] Imam Zufar: wudu breaks before the entry of the time of the obligatory prayer.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the wudu of a mustahadah (a woman with chronic non-menstrual bleeding). The author begins by stating the general ruling: a mustahadah makes wudu at each prayer time, and with that wudu she may pray any number of obligatory and supererogatory prayers until that prayer time exits. When the time exits, her wudu breaks because the need (i.e., the need to maintain purity for the duration of that prayer time) has ended. The blood continues to flow, but due to necessity it was overlooked; once necessity ends, the blood is considered a nullifier. For the next prayer time, she renews her wudu. The proof is the hadith in which the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) instructs the mustahadah to leave prayer during her menses, then bathe and perform wudu for every prayer. Another hadith says to perform wudu for every prayer even if blood drips. The Hanafi understanding is that 'at every prayer' means 'at every prayer time', as is common in Arabic idiom. This brings the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions close to each other. Imam Zufar's opinion (that wudu breaks before the next prayer time) is mentioned but not relied upon.

Key Terms
مستحاضة (mustahādah): A woman who experiences chronic bleeding outside the normal menstrual period (istihadah).
استحاضة (istiḥāḍah): Chronic vaginal bleeding that is not part of menses or postpartum bleeding; it nullifies wudu only when it flows.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the Hanafi madhhab interpret 'at every prayer' (عند كل صلاة) in the hadith regarding the mustahadah?
A) At the time of each individual prayer, so one wudu can cover multiple prayers if performed back-to-back.
B) At each prayer time, so the wudu is valid for the entire time period and breaks only when the time exits.
C) At each prayer, meaning one must renew wudu for every single rak'ah.
D) At each prayer, meaning one must perform ghusl before every prayer.

Key Takeaway
The mustahadah's wudu is valid for an entire prayer time due to the principle of necessity, as derived from the hadith's imperative 'for every prayer' meaning 'for every prayer time'.

Rulings on Nifas: Definition, Limits, and Habit
Definition, limits, and habit-based extension of nifas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of nifas, identify its minimum and maximum limits, and apply the rule when postpartum bleeding exceeds 40 days using the woman's habitual pattern.

Core Idea
Nifas has no minimum, a maximum of 40 days; any excess is istihadah unless a prior habit shortens the period.

Nifas (postpartum bleeding) is a natural condition after childbirth. Understanding its limits and when it transitions to istihadah is crucial for a woman to know when she must resume prayer and fasting. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position on the duration of nifas and how to handle bleeding that exceeds 40 days.

Guided Path
1
Define nifas and distinguish it from istihadah
Identify the source's definition: nifas is blood that emerges after childbirth. Blood during pregnancy or before the baby exits is istihadah, not nifas or menses. This distinction determines the legal rulings for prayer and fasting.
Note the cross-madhhab difference: Shafi'is consider blood during pregnancy as menses.
2
Identify the minimum and maximum limits
The source states the minimum has no limit (even a short flow counts as nifas) and the maximum is 40 days, based on the hadith from Umm Salamah. Bleeding beyond 40 days is istihadah unless habit dictates otherwise.
The maximum is based on practice of the Companions and the Prophet's tacit approval.
3
Apply the rule when blood exceeds 40 days
If bleeding continues past 40 days, check if the woman has a previous nifas habit (e.g., 25 days). If yes, she is in nifas only for her habitual days; the rest is istihadah. If no habit, nifas is exactly 40 days and the rest is istihadah.
The habit is determined from her most recent previous delivery.
4
Understand the practical consequence of the ruling
During istihadah days, the woman is considered ritually pure and must pray and fast like a non-menstruating woman, though she may need to perform wudu for each prayer due to chronic bleeding.
This ruling affects acts of worship during the postpartum period.
